  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_bd53e9d7-dd82-4b92-9cdd-e8d7967741db'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1390541095" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer16195829409569034267">The Sword Thief (The 39 Clues #3), Peter Lerangis<br/>The Sword Thief is the third book in The 39 Clues series. It was written by Peter Lerangis and was published by Scholastic on March 3, 2009. The Sword Thief follows the first two books in the series, The Maze of Bones by Rick Riordan and One False Note by Gordon Korman. The following book continues the plot in Beyond the Grave.<br/>The 39 Clues series: The Sword Thief (March 3, 2009), The Viper's Nest (February 2, 2010), Vespers Rising (April 5, 2011)<br/>ت</span>
  <span id="freeText16195829409569034267" style="display:none">The Sword Thief (The 39 Clues #3), Peter Lerangis<br />The Sword Thief is the third book in The 39 Clues series. It was written by Peter Lerangis and was published by Scholastic on March 3, 2009. The Sword Thief follows the first two books in the series, The Maze of Bones by Rick Riordan and One False Note by Gordon Korman. <div id="review_84888248" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/84888248" />
    <a title="Laura" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1735763-laura"><img alt="Laura" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442495481p2/1735763.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/84888248?book_show_action=true">Jan 13, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura" href="/user/show/1735763-laura">Laura</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer84888248" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5983127057879382169">Unlike The 39 Clues Book Two: One False Note where the action is non-stop, in The 39 Clues Book Three: The Sword Thief things slow down, and we get to know the characters better, especially the rivals, as they pursue their quest for the 39 clues. I liked this change. It reminded me of The 39 Clues Book One: The Maze of Bones, where all the characters were introduced and we got a good picture of them.<br/><br/>With this book the reader gets a chance to get his bearings and to recap all the events that now </span>
  <span id="freeText5983127057879382169" style="display:none">Unlike The 39 Clues Book Two: One False Note where the action is non-stop, in The 39 Clues Book Three: The Sword Thief things slow down, and we get to know the characters better, especially the rivals, as they pursue their quest for the 39 clues. I liked this change. It reminded me of The 39 Clues Book One: The Maze of Bones, where all the characters were introduced and we got a good picture of them.<br /><br />With this book the reader gets a chance to get his bearings and to recap all the events that now leads the brother-and-sister duo team of Amy and Dan Cahill to Japan and Korea. This time we learn about the historical person Toyotomi Hideyoshi who has hidden the third clue. As the hunt moves to the Orient, Amy and Dan have no choice but to enlist the help of their Uncle Alistair Oh. With the one rule of the race being TRUST NO ONE, Amy and Dan must exercise caution as to how much to divulge of their knowledge and exactly how far to trust two of their rival teams—Alistair Oh and the Kabra siblings—while dodging bullets and swords to escape the yakuza who guard what belonged to their ancestor.<br /><br />It’s a psychological game and we are left wondering what is going to happen by the end of the book. I truly felt author Peter Lerangis captured the original voices of Amy and Dan and their vulnerability as orphans who have lost much. For me, this was essential to keep the thread of the story solid. If it had continued with non-stop action as in Book Two, the impetus that keeps the reader emotionally involved would have been lost. By also engaging the emotions of the other rivals instead of just relating their killer actions, the stakes are elevated. And of course, the cliffhanger ending keeps us hungering for more.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5983127057879382169" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_81016170" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/81016170" />
    <a title="Jelinas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2297897-jelinas"><img alt="Jelinas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1324389769p2/2297897.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/81016170?book_show_action=true">Dec 14, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jelinas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jelinas" href="/user/show/2297897-jelinas">Jelinas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer81016170" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17268646149706929569">When I was younger, I used to despise Koreans. I would get really embarrassed when all the other Korean kids started drawing Korean flags on their backpacks and notebooks and writing “KP” (“Korean Pride”) on everything they owned (I still think I was a tiny bit justified in that embarrassment. That’s pretty lame, yo).<br/><br/>But, as I grew older, I realized that, whether I liked it or not, being Korean was part of my heritage, and trying to cut that off was like cutting off my nose to spite my face. I l</span>
  <span id="freeText17268646149706929569" style="display:none">When I was younger, I used to despise Koreans. I would get really embarrassed when all the other Korean kids started drawing Korean flags on their backpacks and notebooks and writing “KP” (“Korean Pride”) on everything they owned (I still think I was a tiny bit justified in that embarrassment. That’s pretty lame, yo).<br /><br />But, as I grew older, I realized that, whether I liked it or not, being Korean was part of my heritage, and trying to cut that off was like cutting off my nose to spite my face. I learned to accept the good things that came with the culture (the emphasis on diligence, integrity, and respect) and to watch out for the bad (arrogance in achievement, undue emphasis on success in the eyes of the world, and the tendency to bury emotions and affection down where no one can find it, not even you).<br /><br />Besides that, I realized that Koreans have the best cuisine in the world. Seriously, Korean food is tha BOMB.<br /><br />And then I read <i>The Sword Thief</i> and now I hate Koreans again.<br /><br />The third book in the <i>39 Clues</i> series (I can’t believe I’m only one-thirteenth of the way through this series. Kill me now) contains two elements that I find absolutely repulsive. The first is a mysterious Korean uncle to two white kids (you still haven’t explained this to my satisfaction, writers), who alternates between wanting to help the kids and wanting to hurt the kids to help himself. Through it all, he’s creepy as creepy gets, and the picture of an actor posing as him on one of the collectible cards was even creepier because the book makes him sound like an old man, but the man in the picture was youngish. Creepy uncle of eternal youth? Creepy.<br /><br />And his name is supposed to be Alistair Oh? Shenanigans. Strike three, writers.<br /><br />The second creepy-as-all-get-out element is Amy Cahill’s crush on her COUSIN. Ew, ew, and EW. I don’t care that they’re probably not that closely related. There’s even a scene where Ian Kabra, the handsome cousin (GAG) “lightly brushes her lips with his” or some crap like that. EEEWWWW!!!! Excuse my while I <i>puke</i>!!! That is so not appropriate, I don’t know what to say. And the worst part of it is that it’s not even being played for laughs, or with any hint of irony. When the writers of “Arrested Development” put romantic tension between George Michael (the adorable Michael Cera) and his cousin Maeby (the also adorable Alia Shawkat), at least there was a chance she was adopted and it was all played for laughs anyway. But <i>The 38 Clues</i> is taking this über-seriously. As serious as the hereditary diseases that result from inbreeding.<br /><br />Other than that, the adventure part of this novel was pretty much the same old schlock I’m getting used to seeing from this series. Kids race around exotic foreign location, avoid baddies, run into baddies, somehow elude their grasp <i>and</i> manage to be the first to find the next clue in the series.<br /><br />Three down. Thirty-six to go. *sigh*</span>
  <a data-text-id="17268646149706929569" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText910737968224133566" style="display:none">I knew it.<br /><br />I loved it.<br /><br />I want to punch them.<br /><br />I wanted Amy and Ian together. I still do. So I don't care about the betrayal (because his face said he didn't really want to do it, at least a bit) or his selfish self or even Jake; I still want them together, I still think she could make him change his mind, I still have him as my favorite character.<br /><br />The development of this book was good, with little action but full-intense moments. It was a great time to get to know the characters better and those about-five-pages-in-total of romance were... Oh My God. Uncle Alistair's disappearence and the kid's reaction to everything felt really sad. I also loved Peter Lerangis's writing style. The way he described people and places was great, so I hope he keeps that for the other book he writes for this series.<br /><br />I'm SO looking forward to buy the next book when it gets to my country.<br /><br />Now, if you'll excuse me, I'll go listen I Knew You Were Trouble by Taylor Swift, Trecherous by her too and Steal Your Heart by Ross Lynch, for Ian and Amy, at least a thousand times.</span>

  <span id="freeText10247639484962646517" style="display:none">Uh... wow. That was a really quick read. Last I checked, I just started leafing through the pages and now I'm done.<br /><br />Peter Lerangis, what have you done to the characters? You somehow tweaked them up a little bit. Since when did Amy begin to stammer so much when she talks? <br /><br />Anyway, what Amy felt for Ian was quite incestuous IMO. They're cousins first of all, unless the bloodline is very distant. And I thought it was pretty clear that she and Dan despise the Kabras so much though it was mentioned earlier that Amy, herself had a teeny tiny crush on her cousin Ian.<br /><br />Unlike the previous books in the series, this had a very few exciting moments. The transitions occurred almost too quickly. It's like one second, they're here and one nanosecond later, they're somewhere else. <br /><br />I'm holding the fourth book right now and I can't wait to read this sometime. It's also quite thicker than the first three books.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4848082906657390469">The Sword Thief follows the Cahills as they travel to their next destination, Japan and not too far behind them are their competitors in the race for the ultimate prize. What the prize is, no one knows yet, but this book will soon reveal the answer. Personally I felt the Sword Thief was a little more rushed than the previous books. I liked the recurring appearances of the siblings' opponents throughout their clue-hunting. I did find the relationship between Ian and Amy off-putting and completely</span>
  <span id="freeText4848082906657390469" style="display:none">The Sword Thief follows the Cahills as they travel to their next destination, Japan and not too far behind them are their competitors in the race for the ultimate prize. What the prize is, no one knows yet, but this book will soon reveal the answer. Personally I felt the Sword Thief was a little more rushed than the previous books. I liked the recurring appearances of the siblings' opponents throughout their clue-hunting. I did find the relationship between Ian and Amy off-putting and completely random. Aren't they meant to be related? (distantly but still, add this to the fact that they're enemies) I like how each 39 clues book finishes with a major cliffhanger. The possibilities are limitless for the next author to continue the story.</span>

Throughout their search in Korea, Amy and Ian start falling for each other. This book is a must read for any 39 Clues fan and this an Amian fan’s favourite book.</span>

<div id="review_3013868050" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3013868050" />
    <a title="Madison H." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/104124564-madison-h"><img alt="Madison H." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571261715p2/104124564.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3013868050?book_show_action=true">Nov 05, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Madison H." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Madison H." href="/user/show/104124564-madison-h">Madison H.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3013868050" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5672104416197025173">I originally decided to read this book series because my favorite author, Rick Riordan, wrote the first one, so naturally, I was intrigued. The book did not disappoint, and it only got better from there. This title has been the best so far, instead of the action-packed normality, we, the readers, got to read more about their characters and background, specifically, Alistair Oh. The story touches on is direct family members of his uncle and dad, talking about his relationship with them and where </span>
  <span id="freeText5672104416197025173" style="display:none">I originally decided to read this book series because my favorite author, Rick Riordan, wrote the first one, so naturally, I was intrigued. The book did not disappoint, and it only got better from there. This title has been the best so far, instead of the action-packed normality, we, the readers, got to read more about their characters and background, specifically, Alistair Oh. The story touches on is direct family members of his uncle and dad, talking about his relationship with them and where they are now. The book usually consists of the competing family members trying to trick and harm each other, which we do get in this book, but not as much of. We get to see more positive interactions between characters and a bit of trust. This is good and all until two characters have a crush on each other that are related. Even though the Cahill family Is very large with many distant relatives, just knowing that they are related and have a crush on each other is just unsettling and uncomfortable. Otherwise it is a great read and I recommend it to anyone that is interested. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5672104416197025173" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_49028918" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/49028918" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/879591-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1207591498p2/879591.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/49028918?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/879591-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/879591-sara?shelf=middle-grade">middle-grade</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/879591-sara?shelf=2009">2009</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer49028918" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17914687221068597189">What struck me about this book before I even started reading it was how short it was compared to the first two books in the series. Then, while I read this, I thought about how the writing in this book isn't nearly as good as the writing in the first two. I know, each book has a different author, but that doesn't mean that even though the writing might be different, it necessarily has to be worse. Unfortunately, I thought that was the case with this book. It didn't flow as nicely, there were min</span>
  <span id="freeText17914687221068597189" style="display:none">What struck me about this book before I even started reading it was how short it was compared to the first two books in the series. Then, while I read this, I thought about how the writing in this book isn't nearly as good as the writing in the first two. I know, each book has a different author, but that doesn't mean that even though the writing might be different, it necessarily has to be worse. Unfortunately, I thought that was the case with this book. It didn't flow as nicely, there were mini-cliffhangers at the end of each chapter (actually, that's a good thing - they just weren't as exciting as they seemed). There was a lot of page-filler material with characters saying dumb things that were supposed to be funny, and I wish the time had been spent instead on increasing the already-short book with a fuller plot. It seemed like it was written by an older person with no idea how kids actually talk, instead throwing in lots of "like"s and "hip-hop music", etc, which made the writing and the story cheesy and dumb instead of funny and genuine.<br /><br />In this installment, Amy and Dan head to Japan, following a new clue they've received, and must decide whether or not to form an alliance with their uncle, Allister Oh, who's betrayed them once... but they're currently facing a bigger betrayal from their cousins, and Allister could prove a good ally.<br /><br />There wasn't as much history talked about in this book as the first two, which was disappointing, as I really liked that aspect of the series. I also was disappointed by how quickly all the new discoveries they made were described; sometimes I even missed big details because they were glossed over so fast in the book. <br /><br />All in all, not the strongest installment. I am hoping the next book is better.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17914687221068597189" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_464487082" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/464487082" />
    <a title="Duffy Pratt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2729963-duffy-pratt"><img alt="Duffy Pratt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1282095213p2/2729963.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/464487082?book_show_action=true">Nov 26, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Duffy Pratt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Duffy Pratt" href="/user/show/2729963-duffy-pratt">Duffy Pratt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2729963-duffy-pratt?shelf=childrens">childrens</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2729963-duffy-pratt?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer464487082" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6768467038857212919">This one was slightly less fun than its predecessors. The go to Japan and then Korea, and end up trapped underground yet again (this is emerging as a motif in these books). A character dies, but doesn't. Lots of threats and danger, but once more no-one gets hurt. Betrayal everywhere. Solutions make no sense, and even when they come, they lead to greater misunderstanding than what they clear up. On that score, it's about par for this series.<br/><br/>There's an anagram at the end of the book. It has many p</span>
  <span id="freeText6768467038857212919" style="display:none">This one was slightly less fun than its predecessors. The go to Japan and then Korea, and end up trapped underground yet again (this is emerging as a motif in these books). A character dies, but doesn't. Lots of threats and danger, but once more no-one gets hurt. Betrayal everywhere. Solutions make no sense, and even when they come, they lead to greater misunderstanding than what they clear up. On that score, it's about par for this series.<br /><br />There's an anagram at the end of the book. It has many permutations that might make sense. Dan sends off the Kabras on one "solution" that is the wrong one. He then declares the "right" solution, which has to do with the secret formula they are after. Amy then says that the right "right" solution is yet another permutation that would take them to Egypt. Why is the Egypt solution better than the wild goose chase solution that Dan gave the Kabras? It's beyond me, but that's just the way this book is. Once again, I'm struck with how this book seems like "The DaVinci Code" for kids, even though I already thought DaVinci Code was childish enough.<br /><br />One interesting aspect of this series is that the books are by different authors. Here, I thought the writing was weaker than in the first two. It might be because some of the running jokes are starting to run out of steam, and it might be because Lerangis is just not as good a writer. If I go on in the series, I might want to pay a little more attention to similarities and differences in the writing styles.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6768467038857212919" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/445052143?book_show_action=true">Oct 30, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Heather" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Heather" href="/user/show/1212796-heather">Heather</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1212796-heather?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1212796-heather?shelf=school-books-kid-books">school-books-kid-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer445052143" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer284931286501634912">I'm going to give a generic review of the series. Basically because I'm lazy and don't want to write 39 reviews. Okay, 11.<br/><br/>We finally read Book 1. And then because we liked it so well, the entire series. One son (age 12) has read them all, the second son (also age 12) is on Book 2. And son #3 (age 8) will be starting as soon as he finishes his current book.<br/><br/>I thought the premise of this book very interesting. I loved how they integrated history and geography along with problem-solving skills and c</span>
  <span id="freeText284931286501634912" style="display:none">I'm going to give a generic review of the series. Basically because I'm lazy and don't want to write 39 reviews. Okay, 11.<br /><br />We finally read Book 1. And then because we liked it so well, the entire series. One son (age 12) has read them all, the second son (also age 12) is on Book 2. And son #3 (age 8) will be starting as soon as he finishes his current book.<br /><br />I thought the premise of this book very interesting. I loved how they integrated history and geography along with problem-solving skills and code cracking. Add in the action, humor and intrigue and kids (and some adults) can't put them down. All three of us that have read 39 Clues thought Book 2 the weakest of the series. But as one son said, after finishing the series, "Gordon Korman seems to be getting better. I think he's a decent author now." <br /><br />Some of the books are better at teaching the history part, others just barely skim the surface and you don't really learn about the person they're 'highlighting'. I appreciate that they're clean books - no curse words or you-know-what going on. There is some violence (very little, if any, blood) and several murder attempts, but they never work.<br /><br />The series would most likely be called twaddle by my Charlotte Mason friends. I do, however, think it has more redeeming qualities than most kids literature and I'm happy to see my boys devouring a book every day or so.</span>
  <a data-text-id="284931286501634912" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('5Wl1fuuy+xdqW3NhE2p3UBCPNtdrL1+x49tQbZ3J3EWse3pyvDTi4CcKxPVSd1ZZPocqoQLPNk/D+AfwDKAG9g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('c+dXdLqGhn4NJhHOVszmpA4pHjIjSjytQ/AMlaBcj1g69Vh47QCfiUB3ploX0cetICECREqqVVNj01sIMTVV6w==')})
  }
//]]>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/17216.Peter_Lerangis">About Peter Lerangis</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/17216.Peter_Lerangis"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1273412599p3/17216.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/17216.Peter_Lerangis">Peter Lerangis
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
701
followers

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer989600545765654342">Lerangis's work includes The Viper's Nest and The Sword Thief, two titles in the children's-book series The 39 Clues, the historical novel Smiler's Bones, the YA dark comedy-adventure novel wtf, the Drama Club series, the Spy X series, the Watchers series, the Abracadabra series, and the Antarctica two-book adventure, as well ghostwriting for series such as the Three Investigators, the Hardy Boys </span>
  <span id="freeText989600545765654342" style="display:none">Lerangis's work includes The Viper's Nest and The Sword Thief, two titles in the children's-book series The 39 Clues, the historical novel Smiler's Bones, the YA dark comedy-adventure novel wtf, the Drama Club series, the Spy X series, the Watchers series, the Abracadabra series, and the Antarctica two-book adventure, as well ghostwriting for series such as the Three Investigators, the Hardy Boys Casefiles, Sweet Valley Twins, and more than forty books in the series The Baby-sitters Club and its various spin-offs.[1] He has also written novels based on film screenplays, including The Sixth Sense, Sleepy Hollow, and Beauty and the Beast, and five video game novelizations in the Worlds of Power series created by Seth Godin.[2] As a ghostwriter he has been published under the name A. L. Singer.[3]<br />Lerangis is the son of a retired New York Telephone Company employee and a retired public-elementary-school secretary, who raised him in Freeport, New York on Long Island. He graduated from Harvard University with a degree in biochemistry, while acting in musicals[4] and singing with and musically directing the a cappella group the Harvard Krokodiloes,[5][6] before moving to New York. He worked there as an actor[7] and freelance copy editor for eight years before becoming an author.[8]<br />In 2003, Lerangis was chosen by First Lady Laura Bush to accompany her to the first Russian Book Festival, hosted by Russian First Lady Lyudmila Putina in Moscow.[9][10]Authors R. L. Stine (Goosebumps) and Marc Brown (the Arthur the Aardvark series) also made the trip with Bush.[9]<br />Also in 2003, Lerangis was commissioned by the United Kingdom branch of Scholastic to write X-Isle, one of four books that would relaunch the Point Horror series there.[11] A sequel, Return to X-Isle, was published in 2004.<br />In 2007, Scholastic announced the launch of a new historical mystery series called The 39 Clues, intended to become a franchise.[12] Lerangis wrote the third book in the series, The Sword Thief, published in March 2009.[13][14][15] On March 3, 2009, Scholastic announced that Lerangis would write the seventh book in the series, The Viper's Nest.[14][16]<br />Lerangis lives in New York City with his wife, musician Tina deVaron, and their sons Nick and Joe.[17]</span>
  <a data-text-id="989600545765654342" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/6034082-the-sword-thief">Quotes from The Sword Thief</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;I g-g-guess...I'm dead?" she heard her own voice call out, strangely high-pitched and thin.<br />For a long time, she heard nothing else. And then:<br />"Hi, Dead. I'm Dan.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/368872-i-g-g-guess-i-m-dead-she-heard-her-own-voice-call-out">94 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;<i>GGRRROOCCCCK...</i><br />Ian's knees buckled. The rock outcropping shook the ground, sending a spew of grayish dust that quickly billowed around them.<br />Shielding his eyes, he spotted Amy standing by the figurine, which was now moving toward her. She was in shock, her backpack on the ground by her feet. <br />"Get back!" he shouted. <br />Ian pulled Amy away and threw her to the ground, landing on top of her. Gravel showered over his back, embedding into his hair and landing on the ground like a burst of applause. <br />His second though was that the shirt would be ruined. And this was the shock of it-that his first thought had not been about the shirt. Or the coin. Or himself. <br />It had been about <i>her</i>.<br />But that was not part of the plan. She existed for a purpose. She was a tactic, a stepping stone. She was...<br />"Lovely," he said. <br />Amy was staring up at him, petrified, her eyelashes flecked with dust. Ian took her hand, which was knotted into a fist. "Y-y-you don't have to do that," she whispered. <br />"Do what?" Ian asked. <br />"Be sarcastic. Say things like 'lovely.' You saved my life. Th-thank you."<br />"My duty," he replied. He lowered his head and allowed his lips to brush hers. Just a bit.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/242419-ggrrroocccck-ian-s-knees-buckled-the-rock-outcropping-shook-the-ground">69 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
